class="sidebar-link">Website</a></li></ul></li></ul></section></li></ul> </aside> <main class="page"> <div class="theme-default-content content__default"><h2 id="introduction"><a href="#introduction" class="header-anchor">#</a> Introduction</h2> <p>The <a href="https://www.artic.edu" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Art Institute of Chicago<span><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" x="0px" y="0px" viewBox="0 0 100 100" width="15" height="15" class="icon outbound"><path fill="currentColor" d="M18.8,85.1h56l0,0c2.2,0,4-1.8,4-4v-32h-8v28h-48v-48h28v-8h-32l0,0c-2.2,0-4,1.8-4,4v56C14.8,83.3,16.6,85.1,18.8,85.1z"></path> <polygon fill="currentColor" points="45.7,48.7 51.3,54.3 77.2,28.5 77.2,37.2 85.2,37.2 85.2,14.9 62.8,14.9 62.8,22.9 71.5,22.9"></polygon></svg> <span class="sr-only">(opens new window)</span></span></a>'s API provides JSON-formatted data as a REST-style service that allows developers to explore and integrate the museum’s public data into their projects. This API is the same tool that powers our <a href="https://www.artic.edu" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">website<span><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" x="0px" y="0px" viewBox="0 0 100 100" width="15" height="15" class="icon outbound"><path fill="currentColor" d="M18.8,85.1h56l0,0c2.2,0,4-1.8,4-4v-32h-8v28h-48v-48h28v-8h-32l0,0c-2.2,0-4,1.8-4,4v56C14.8,83.3,16.6,85.1,18.8,85.1z"></path> <polygon fill="currentColor" points="45.7,48.7 51.3,54.3 77.2,28.5 77.2,37.2 85.2,37.2 85.2,14.9 62.8,14.9 62.8,22.9 71.5,22.9"></polygon></svg> <span class="sr-only">(opens new window)</span></span></a>, our <a href="https://www.artic.edu/visit/explore-on-your-own/mobile-app-audio-tours" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">mobile app<span><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" x="0px" y="0px" viewBox="0 0 100 100" width="15" height="15" class="icon outbound"><path fill="currentColor" d="M18.8,85.1h56l0,0c2.2,0,4-1.8,4-4v-32h-8v28h-48v-48h28v-8h-32l0,0c-2.2,0-4,1.8-4,4v56C14.8,83.3,16.6,85.1,18.8,85.1z"></path> <polygon fill="currentColor" points="45.7,48.7 51.3,54.3 77.2,28.5 77.2,37.2 85.2,37.2 85.2,14.9 62.8,14.9 62.8,22.9 71.5,22.9"></polygon></svg> <span class="sr-only">(opens new window)</span></span></a>, and many other technologies in the museum.</p> <p>If the application you're building will be public, please send it our way! We'd love to share it alongside some of the <a href="https://www.artic.edu/open-access/public-api" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">other projects that use our API<span><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" x="0px" y="0px" viewBox="0 0 100 100" width="15" height="15" class="icon outbound"><path fill="currentColor" d="M18.8,85.1h56l0,0c2.2,0,4-1.8,4-4v-32h-8v28h-48v-48h28v-8h-32l0,0c-2.2,0-4,1.8-4,4v56C14.8,83.3,16.6,85.1,18.8,85.1z"></path> <polygon fill="currentColor" points="45.7,48.7 51.3,54.3 77.2,28.5 77.2,37.2 85.2,37.2 85.2,14.9 62.8,14.9 62.8,22.9 71.5,22.9"></polygon></svg> <span class="sr-only">(opens new window)</span></span></a>. And if you have any questions, please feel free to reach out to us: <a href="mailto:engineering@artic.edu">engineering@artic.edu</a>.</p> <h3 id="quick-start"><a href="#quick-start" class="header-anchor">#</a> Quick Start</h3> <p>An <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=81ygVYCupdo" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">API<span><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" x="0px" y="0px" viewBox="0 0 100 100" width="15" height="15" class="icon outbound"><path fill="currentColor" d="M18.8,85.1h56l0,0c2.2,0,4-1.8,4-4v-32h-8v28h-48v-48h28v-8h-32l0,0c-2.2,0-4,1.8-4,4v56C14.8,83.3,16.6,85.1,18.8,85.1z"></path> <polygon fill="currentColor" points="45.7,48.7 51.3,54.3 77.2,28.5 77.2,37.2 85.2,37.2 85.2,14.9 62.8,14.9 62.8,22.9 71.5,22.9"></polygon></svg> <span class="sr-only">(opens new window)</span></span></a> is a structured way that one software application can talk to another. APIs power much of the software we use today, from the apps on our phones and watches to technology we see in sports and TV shows. We have built an API to let people like you easily get our data in an ongoing way.</p> <div class="custom-block tip"><p class="custom-block-title">TIP</p> <p>Do you want <em>all</em> of our data? Are you running into problems with <a href="#authentication">throttling</a> or <a href="#pagination">deep pagination</a>? Consider using our <a href="#data-dumps">data dumps</a> instead of our API.</p></div> <p>For example, you can access the <code>/artworks</code> listing endpoint in our API by visiting the following URL to see all the published artworks in our collection:</p> <div class="language- extra-class"><pre class="language-text"><code>https://api.artic.edu/api/v1/artworks
</code></pre></div><p>If you want to see data for just one artwork, you can use the <code>/artworks/{id}</code> detail endpoint. For example, here's <a href="https://www.artic.edu/artworks/129884/starry-night-and-the-astronauts" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"><em>Starry Night and the Astronauts</em><span><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" x="0px" y="0px" viewBox="0 0 100 100" width="15" height="15" class="icon outbound"><path fill="currentColor" d="M18.8,85.1h56l0,0c2.2,0,4-1.8,4-4v-32h-8v28h-48v-48h28v-8h-32l0,0c-2.2,0-4,1.8-4,4v56C14.8,83.3,16.6,85.1,18.8,85.1z"></path> <polygon fill="currentColor" points="45.7,48.7 51.3,54.3 77.2,28.5 77.2,37.2 85.2,37.2 85.2,14.9 62.8,14.9 62.8,22.9 71.5,22.9"></polygon></svg> <span class="sr-only">(opens new window)</span></span></a> by Alma Thomas:</p> <div class="language- extra-class"><pre class="language-text"><code>https://api.artic.edu/api/v1/artworks/129884
</code></pre></div><p>When you view these URLs in your browser, you might see a jumbled bunch of text. That's OK! If you're using Chrome, install the <a href="https://chrome.google.com/webstore/detail/json-formatter/bcjindcccaagfpapjjmafapmmgkkhgoa" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">JSON Formatter extension<span><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" x="0px" y="0px" viewBox="0 0 100 100" width="15" height="15" class="icon outbound"><path fill="currentColor" d="M18.8,85.1h56l0,0c2.2,0,4-1.8,4-4v-32h-8v28h-48v-48h28v-8h-32l0,0c-2.2,0-4,1.8-4,4v56C14.8,83.3,16.6,85.1,18.8,85.1z"></path> <polygon fill="currentColor" points="45.7,48.7 51.3,54.3 77.2,28.5 77.2,37.2 85.2,37.2 85.2,14.9 62.8,14.9 62.8,22.9 71.5,22.9"></polygon></svg> <span class="sr-only">(opens new window)</span></span></a>, hit reload, and the results will be formatted in a way humans can read, too.</p> <p>There is a lot of data you'll get for each artwork. If you want to only retrieve a certain set of fields, change the <code>fields</code> parameter in the query to list which ones you want, like this:</p> <div class="language- extra-class"><pre class="language-text"><code>https://api.artic.edu/api/v1/artworks?fields=id,title,artist_display,date_display,main_reference_number
</code></pre></div><div class="custom-block tip"><p class="custom-block-title">TIP</p> <p>The <code>fields</code> parameter expects either an array of field names, or a comma-separated list of field names in a single string (e.g. example above). We encourage you to use it because it makes your queries run faster and lets us track which fields need continued support.</p></div> <p>You can <a href="#pagination">paginate</a> through results using <code>page</code> and <code>limit</code> params:</p> <div class="language- extra-class"><pre class="language-text"><code>https://api.artic.edu/api/v1/artworks?page=2&amp;limit=100
</code></pre></div><p>If you want to search and filter the results, you can do so via our search endpoints. For example, here is a <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Full-text_search" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">full-text search<span><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" x="0px" y="0px" viewBox="0 0 100 100" width="15" height="15" class="icon outbound"><path fill="currentColor" d="M18.8,85.1h56l0,0c2.2,0,4-1.8,4-4v-32h-8v28h-48v-48h28v-8h-32l0,0c-2.2,0-4,1.8-4,4v56C14.8,83.3,16.6,85.1,18.8,85.1z"></path> <polygon fill="currentColor" points="45.7,48.7 51.3,54.3 77.2,28.5 77.2,37.2 85.2,37.2 85.2,14.9 62.8,14.9 62.8,22.9 71.5,22.9"></polygon></svg> <span class="sr-only">(opens new window)</span></span></a> for all artworks whose metadata contains some mention of cats:</p> <div class="language- extra-class"><pre class="language-text"><code>https://api.artic.edu/api/v1/artworks/search?q=cats
</code></pre></div><p>Here is the same search, but filtered to only show artworks that are in the public domain:</p> <div class="language- extra-class"><pre class="language-text"><code>https://api.artic.edu/api/v1/artworks/search?q=cats&amp;query[term][is_public_domain]=true
</code></pre></div><p>Behind the scenes, our search is powered by <a href="https://www.elastic.co/what-is/elasticsearch" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Elasticsearch<span><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" x="0px" y="0px" viewBox="0 0 100 100" width="15" height="15" class="icon outbound"><path fill="currentColor" d="M18.8,85.1h56l0,0c2.2,0,4-1.8,4-4v-32h-8v28h-48v-48h28v-8h-32l0,0c-2.2,0-4,1.8-4,4v56C14.8,83.3,16.6,85.1,18.8,85.1z"></path> <polygon fill="currentColor" points="45.7,48.7 51.3,54.3 77.2,28.5 77.2,37.2 85.2,37.2 85.2,14.9 62.8,14.9 62.8,22.9 71.5,22.9"></polygon></svg> <span class="sr-only">(opens new window)</span></span></a>. You can use its <a href="https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/elasticsearch/reference/current/query-dsl.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Query DSL<span><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" x="0px" y="0px" viewBox="0 0 100 100" width="15" height="15" class="icon outbound"><path fill="currentColor" d="M18.8,85.1h56l0,0c2.2,0,4-1.8,4-4v-32h-8v28h-48v-48h28v-8h-32l0,0c-2.2,0-4,1.8-4,4v56C14.8,83.3,16.6,85.1,18.8,85.1z"></path> <polygon fill="currentColor" points="45.7,48.7 51.3,54.3 77.2,28.5 77.2,37.2 85.2,37.2 85.2,14.9 62.8,14.9 62.8,22.9 71.5,22.9"></polygon></svg> <span class="sr-only">(opens new window)</span></span></a> to interact with our API. The example above uses a <a href="https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/elasticsearch/reference/current/query-dsl-term-query.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"><code>term</code><span><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" x="0px" y="0px" viewBox="0 0 100 100" width="15" height="15" class="icon outbound"><path fill="currentColor" d="M18.8,85.1h56l0,0c2.2,0,4-1.8,4-4v-32h-8v28h-48v-48h28v-8h-32l0,0c-2.2,0-4,1.8-4,4v56C14.8,83.3,16.6,85.1,18.8,85.1z"></path> <polygon fill="currentColor" points="45.7,48.7 51.3,54.3 77.2,28.5 77.2,37.2 85.2,37.2 85.2,14.9 62.8,14.9 62.8,22.9 71.5,22.9"></polygon></svg> <span class="sr-only">(opens new window)</span></span></a> query. Other queries we use often include <a href="https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/elasticsearch/reference/current/query-dsl-exists-query.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"><code>exists</code><span><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" x="0px" y="0px" viewBox="0 0 100 100" width="15" height="15" class="icon outbound"><path fill="currentColor" d="M18.8,85.1h56l0,0c2.2,0,4-1.8,4-4v-32h-8v28h-48v-48h28v-8h-32l0,0c-2.2,0-4,1.8-4,4v56C14.8,83.3,16.6,85.1,18.8,85.1z"></path> <polygon fill="currentColor" points="45.7,48.7 51.3,54.3 77.2,28.5 77.2,37.2 85.2,37.2 85.2,14.9 62.8,14.9 62.8,22.9 71.5,22.9"></polygon></svg> <span class="sr-only">(opens new window)</span></span></a> and <a href="https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/elasticsearch/reference/current/query-dsl-bool-query.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"><code>bool</code><span><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" x="0px" y="0px" viewBox="0 0 100 100" width="15" height="15" class="icon outbound"><path fill="currentColor" d="M18.8,85.1h56l0,0c2.2,0,4-1.8,4-4v-32h-8v28h-48v-48h28v-8h-32l0,0c-2.2,0-4,1.8-4,4v56C14.8,83.3,16.6,85.1,18.8,85.1z"></path> <polygon fill="currentColor" points="45.7,48.7 51.3,54.3 77.2,28.5 77.2,37.2 85.2,37.2 85.2,14.9 62.8,14.9 62.8,22.9 71.5,22.9"></polygon></svg> <span class="sr-only">(opens new window)</span></span></a>. <a href="https://www.elastic.co/guide/en/elasticsearch/reference/current/search-aggregations.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Aggregations<span><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" x="0px" y="0px" viewBox="0 0 100 100" width="15" height="15" class="icon outbound"><path fill="currentColor" d="M18.8,85.1h56l0,0c2.2,0,4-1.8,4-4v-32h-8v28h-48v-48h28v-8h-32l0,0c-2.2,0-4,1.8-4,4v56C14.8,83.3,16.6,85.1,18.8,85.1z"></path> <polygon fill="currentColor" points="45.7,48.7 51.3,54.3 77.2,28.5 77.2,37.2 85.2,37.2 85.2,14.9 62.8,14.9 62.8,22.9 71.5,22.9"></polygon></svg> <span class="sr-only">(opens new window)</span></span></a> are also a powerful tool.</p> <p>Our API accepts queries through both <code>GET</code> and <code>POST</code>. It might be easier to visualize how the <code>GET</code> query above is structured by comparing it to its <code>POST</code> equivallent:</p> <div class="language-bash extra-class"><pre class="language-bash"><code><span class="token function">curl</span> --location --request POST <span class="token string">'https://api.artic.edu/api/v1/artworks/search'</span> <span class="token punctuation">\</span>
--header <span class="token string">'Content-Type: application/json'</span> <span class="token punctuation">\</span>
--data-raw <span class="token string">'{
    &quot;q&quot;: &quot;cats&quot;,
    &quot;query&quot;: {
        &quot;term&quot;: {
            &quot;is_public_domain&quot;: true
        }
    }
}'</span>
</code></pre></div><p>For production use, we recommend using <code>GET</code> and passing the entire query as minified URL-encoded JSON via the <code>params</code> parameter. For example:</p> <div class="language- extra-class"><pre class="language-text"><code>https://api.artic.edu/api/v1/artworks/search?params=%7B%22q%22%3A%22cats%22%2C%22query%22%3A%7B%22term%22%3A%7B%22is_public_domain%22%3Atrue%7D%7D%7D
</code></pre></div><div class="custom-block warning"><p class="custom-block-title">WARNING</p> <p>Our API is flexible in how it accepts queries, but each method of querying is meant for a specific purpose. See <a href="#get-vs-post">GET vs. POST</a> for more details.</p></div> <p>There's a lot of information you can get about our collection, and there's a lot more than artworks in our API. Explore our documentation to learn more!</p> <h3 id="conventions"><a href="#conventions" class="header-anchor">#</a> Conventions</h3> <ul><li><p>We refer to models in our API as &quot;resources&quot; (e.g. <code>artworks</code>, <code>artists</code>, <code>places</code>)</p></li> <li><p>Resources are typically accessed via their endpoints. Each resource has three endpoints:</p> <ul><li>Listing (e.g. <code>/artworks</code>)</li> <li>Detail (e.g. <code>/artworks/{id}</code>)</li> <li>Search (e.g. <code>/artworks/search</code>) (optional)</li></ul> <p>Some resources might lack a search endpoint if there appears to be no need for it yet.</p></li> <li><p>Multi-word endpoints are hyphenated (e.g. <code>/tour-stop</code>).</p></li> <li><p>All field names are lowercase and snake case (underscore case).</p></li> <li><p>Every resource in the API has <code>id</code> and <code>title</code> fields.</p></li> <li><p>Fields that contain a single id reference to another resource are singular and end with <code>_id</code>:</p> <div class="language-json extra-class"><pre class="language-json"><code><span class="token property">&quot;artist_id&quot;</span><span class="token operator">:</span> <span class="token number">51349</span><span class="token punctuation">,</span>
</code></pre></div></li> <li><p>Fields that contain id references to multiple records from another resource are singular and end with <code>_ids</code>:</p> <div class="language-json extra-class"><pre class="language-json"><code><span class="token property">&quot;style_ids&quot;</span><span class="token operator">:</span> <span class="token punctuation">[</span><span class="token string">&quot;TM-4439&quot;</span><span class="token punctuation">,</span> <span class="token string">&quot;TM-8542&quot;</span><span class="token punctuation">,</span> <span class="token string">&quot;TM-4441&quot;</span><span class="token punctuation">]</span><span class="token punctuation">,</span>
</code></pre></div></li> <li><p>Fields that contain title references to records from other resources follow naming conventions similar to id-based fields:</p> <div class="language-json extra-class"><pre class="language-json"><code><span class="token property">&quot;artist_title&quot;</span><span class="token operator">:</span> <span class="token string">&quot;Ancient Roman&quot;</span><span class="token punctuation">,</span>
<span class="token property">&quot;classification_titles&quot;</span><span class="token operator">:</span> <span class="token punctuation">[</span><span class="token string">&quot;modern and contemporary art&quot;</span><span class="token punctuation">,</span> <span class="token string">&quot;painting&quot;</span><span class="token punctuation">]</span><span class="token punctuation">,</span>
</code></pre></div></li> <li><p>Every title-based field has a <code>keyword</code> subfield in Elasticsearch, which is meant for filters and aggregations. See &quot;New defaults&quot; section in <a href="https://www.elastic.co/blog/strings-are-dead-long-live-strings" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">this article<span><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" x="0px" y="0px" viewBox="0 0 100 100" width="15" height="15" class="icon outbound"><path fill="currentColor" d="M18.8,85.1h56l0,0c2.2,0,4-1.8,4-4v-32h-8v28h-48v-48h28v-8h-32l0,0c-2.2,0-4,1.8-4,4v56C14.8,83.3,16.6,85.1,18.8,85.1z"></path> <polygon fill="currentColor" points="45.7,48.7 51.3,54.3 77.2,28.5 77.2,37.2 85.2,37.2 85.2,14.9 62.8,14.9 62.8,22.9 71.5,22.9"></polygon></svg> <span class="sr-only">(opens new window)</span></span></a> for more info.</p></li> <li><p>Numeric and boolean fields get parsed into actual numbers and booleans:</p> <div class="language-json extra-class"><pre class="language-json"><code><span class="token property">&quot;artwork_id&quot;</span><span class="token operator">:</span> <span class="token string">&quot;45&quot;</span><span class="token punctuation">,</span> <span class="token comment">// BAD</span>
<span class="token property">&quot;date_start&quot;</span><span class="token operator">:</span> <span class="token number">1942</span><span class="token punctuation">,</span> <span class="token comment">// GOOD</span>

<span class="token property">&quot;is_preferred&quot;</span><span class="token operator">:</span> <span class="token string">&quot;True&quot;</span><span class="token punctuation">,</span> <span class="token comment">// BAD</span>
<span class="token property">&quot;is_in_gallery&quot;</span><span class="token operator">:</span> <span class="token boolean">true</span><span class="token punctuation">,</span> <span class="token comment">// GOOD</span>
</code></pre></div></li> <li><p>We never show any empty strings in the API. We only show <code>null</code>:</p> <div class="language-json extra-class"><pre class="language-json"><code><span class="token property">&quot;date_display&quot;</span><span class="token operator">:</span> <span class="token string">&quot;&quot;</span><span class="token punctuation">,</span> <span class="token comment">// BAD</span>
<span class="token property">&quot;artist_display&quot;</span><span class="token operator">:</span> <span class="token null keyword">null</span><span class="token punctuation">,</span> <span class="token comment">// GOOD</span>
</code></pre></div></li> <li><p>We prefer to always show all fields, even if they are <code>null</code> for the current record:</p> <ul><li><p>If a field that typically returns a string, number, or object is empty for a given record, we return it as <code>null</code>, rather than omitting it.</p></li> <li><p>If a field typically returns an array, we prefer to return an empty array, rather than returning <code>null</code>. This is done in part for backwards-compatibility reasons.</p></li></ul></li></ul> <h3 id="best-practices"><a href="#best-practices" class="header-anchor">#</a> Best Practices</h3> <p>Here are some tips that will make your application run faster and/or reduce load on our systems:</p> <ul><li><p>Cache API responses in your system when possible.</p></li> <li><p>Use the <code>fields</code> parameter to tell us exactly what fields you need.</p></li> <li><p>Batch detail queries with the multi-id parameter (<code>?ids=</code>).</p></li> <li><p>Batch search queries with <a href="#multi-search">multi-search</a> (<code>/msearch</code>).</p></li> <li><p>When <a href="#images">downloading images</a>, use <code>/full/843,/0/default.jpg</code> parameters.</p></li> <li><p>When scraping, please use a single thread and self-throttle.</p></li> <li><p>Consider using <a href="#data-dumps">data dumps</a> instead of scraping our API.</p></li></ul> <h2 id="data-dumps"><a href="#data-dumps" class="header-anchor">#</a> Data Dumps</h2> <p>You can download a dump that contains all of our public data here:</p> <p><a href="https://github.com/art-institute-of-chicago/api-data" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">https://github.com/art-institute-of-chicago/api-data<span><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" x="0px" y="0px" viewBox="0 0 100 100" width="15" height="15" class="icon outbound"><path fill="currentColor" d="M18.8,85.1h56l0,0c2.2,0,4-1.8,4-4v-32h-8v28h-48v-48h28v-8h-32l0,0c-2.2,0-4,1.8-4,4v56C14.8,83.3,16.6,85.1,18.8,85.1z"></path> <polygon fill="currentColor" points="45.7,48.7 51.3,54.3 77.2,28.5 77.2,37.2 85.2,37.2 85.2,14.9 62.8,14.9 62.8,22.9 71.5,22.9"></polygon></svg> <span class="sr-only">(opens new window)</span></span></a></p> <p>These data dumps are updated nightly. They are generated from our API. As such, they contain the same data as our API, and their schema mirrors that of the API. The data is dumped in JSON format, with one JSON file per record. Records are grouped by API resource type.</p> <p>Our intention with this approach is to make it easier to adapt code to draw from the data dumps instead of the API, and vice-versa. Since the schema is the same, switching between the two should be relatively straight-forward.</p> <p>If you notice schema discrepancies between the data dumps and the API, or if you need help with using our data dumps, please <a href="https://github.com/art-institute-of-chicago/api-data/issues" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">open an issue<span><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" x="0px" y="0px" viewBox="0 0 100 100" width="15" height="15" class="icon outbound"><path fill="currentColor" d="M18.8,85.1h56l0,0c2.2,0,4-1.8,4-4v-32h-8v28h-48v-48h28v-8h-32l0,0c-2.2,0-4,1.8-4,4v56C14.8,83.3,16.6,85.1,18.8,85.1z"></path> <polygon fill="currentColor" points="45.7,48.7 51.3,54.3 77.2,28.5 77.2,37.2 85.2,37.2 85.2,14.9 62.8,14.9 62.8,22.9 71.5,22.9"></polygon></svg> <span class="sr-only">(opens new window)</span></span></a> in the <code>api-data</code> repo.</p> <h3 id="data-dumps-vs-api"><a href="#data-dumps-vs-api" class="header-anchor">#</a> Data Dumps vs. API?</h3> <p>We recommend using the data dumps for scenarios such as the following:</p> <ul><li>You want to have a full copy of our data for archival purposes.</li> <li>You want to scrape a large result set (&gt;10,000 records).</li> <li>You want to analyze or enhance our data.</li></ul> <p>Use our API if the following fits your use case:</p> <ul><li>You want to integrate our data into a website or application.</li> <li>You need real-time access to our data.</li></ul> <h3 id="scraping-data"><a href="#scraping-data" class="header-anchor">#</a> Scraping Data</h3> <p>Generally, we ask that you avoid extensive scraping of our API. The API is meant for direct integration into websites and applications. The data dumps are meant for backups and analysis. Scraping our API puts undue stress on our systems. Instead of scraping, please download the data dumps and filter them locally to obtain the data you need.</p> <p>That said, we don't mind small-scale scraping of our API. It can be convenient to use our search endpoint to filter records and retrieve only the fields you need. You can even use the <a href="#aggregations">aggregation</a> functionality of our search endpoints to run some simple analyses. Just remember that you cannot paginate beyond 10,000 results in our search endpoints (see <a href="#pagination">Pagination</a>).</p> <p>If you do decide to scrape our resources, please throttle your requests to no more than one per second and avoid running multiple scrapers in parallel.</p> <h2 id="images"><a href="#images" class="header-anchor">#</a> Images</h2> <p>This API does not contain image files. However, it does contain all of the data you need to access our images. Our institution serves images via a separate API that is compliant with the <a href="https://iiif.io/api/image/2.0/" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">IIIF Image API 2.0<span><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" x="0px" y="0px" viewBox="0 0 100 100" width="15" height="15" class="icon outbound"><path fill="currentColor" d="M18.8,85.1h56l0,0c2.2,0,4-1.8,4-4v-32h-8v28h-48v-48h28v-8h-32l0,0c-2.2,0-4,1.8-4,4v56C14.8,83.3,16.6,85.1,18.8,85.1z"></path> <polygon fill="currentColor" points="45.7,48.7 51.3,54.3 77.2,28.5 77.2,37.2 85.2,37.2 85.2,14.9 62.8,14.9 62.8,22.9 71.5,22.9"></polygon></svg> <span class="sr-only">(opens new window)</span></span></a> specification. Using metadata from this API, you can craft URLs that will allow you to access images of artworks from our collection.</p> <p>The <a href="https://iiif.io/" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">International Image Interoperability Framework (IIIF)<span><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" x="0px" y="0px" viewBox="0 0 100 100" width="15" height="15" class="icon outbound"><path fill="currentColor" d="M18.8,85.1h56l0,0c2.2,0,4-1.8,4-4v-32h-8v28h-48v-48h28v-8h-32l0,0c-2.2,0-4,1.8-4,4v56C14.8,83.3,16.6,85.1,18.8,85.1z"></path> <polygon fill="currentColor" points="45.7,48.7 51.3,54.3 77.2,28.5 77.2,37.2 85.2,37.2 85.2,14.9 62.8,14.9 62.8,22.9 71.5,22.9"></polygon></svg> <span class="sr-only">(opens new window)</span></span></a> stewards a set of open standards that enables rich access to digital media from libraries, archives, museums, and other cultural institutions around the world. In practical terms, they define several API specifications that enable interoperability. When a tool is built to be IIIF-compliant, it's easier to adapt it to consume images from any number of institutions that offer IIIF-compliant APIs.</p> <h3 id="iiif-image-api"><a href="#iiif-image-api" class="header-anchor">#</a> IIIF Image API</h3> <p>We deliver our images via the <a href="https://iiif.io/api/image/2.0/" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">IIIF Image API 2.0<span><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" x="0px" y="0px" viewBox="0 0 100 100" width="15" height="15" class="icon outbound"><path fill="currentColor" d="M18.8,85.1h56l0,0c2.2,0,4-1.8,4-4v-32h-8v28h-48v-48h28v-8h-32l0,0c-2.2,0-4,1.8-4,4v56C14.8,83.3,16.6,85.1,18.8,85.1z"></path> <polygon fill="currentColor" points="45.7,48.7 51.3,54.3 77.2,28.5 77.2,37.2 85.2,37.2 85.2,14.9 62.8,14.9 62.8,22.9 71.5,22.9"></polygon></svg> <span class="sr-only">(opens new window)</span></span></a>. Our IIIF URLs have the following structure:</p> <div class="language- extra-class"><pre class="language-text"><code>https://www.artic.edu/iiif/2/{identifier}/{region}/{size}/{rotation}/{quality}.{format}
</code></pre></div><p>We recommend the following URL structure for most use-cases:</p> <div class="language- extra-class"><pre class="language-text"><code>https://www.artic.edu/iiif/2/{identifier}/full/843,/0/default.jpg
</code></pre></div><p>Let's jump right into an example. Here's how you can construct IIIF URLs:</p> <ol><li><p>Retrieve one or more artworks with <code>image_id</code> fields. Here are a few ways of doing so:</p> <div class="language- extra-class"><pre class="language-text"><code># La Grande Jatte
https://api.artic.edu/api/v1/artworks/27992?fields=id,title,image_id

# La Grande Jatte and The Bedroom
https://api.artic.edu/api/v1/artworks?ids=27992,28560&amp;fields=id,title,image_id

# Top two public domain artworks
https://api.artic.edu/api/v1/artworks/search?query[term][is_public_domain]=true&amp;limit=2&amp;fields=id,title,image_id
</code></pre></div><p>Let's go with the first one, <a href="https://www.artic.edu/artworks/27992/a-sunday-on-la-grande-jatte-1884" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"><em>La Grande Jatte</em><span><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" x="0px" y="0px" viewBox="0 0 100 100" width="15" height="15" class="icon outbound"><path fill="currentColor" d="M18.8,85.1h56l0,0c2.2,0,4-1.8,4-4v-32h-8v28h-48v-48h28v-8h-32l0,0c-2.2,0-4,1.8-4,4v56C14.8,83.3,16.6,85.1,18.8,85.1z"></path> <polygon fill="currentColor" points="45.7,48.7 51.3,54.3 77.2,28.5 77.2,37.2 85.2,37.2 85.2,14.9 62.8,14.9 62.8,22.9 71.5,22.9"></polygon></svg> <span class="sr-only">(opens new window)</span></span></a>. Your response will look something like this:</p> <div class="language- extra-class"><pre class="language-text"><code>{
    &quot;data&quot;: {
        &quot;id&quot;: 27992,
        &quot;title&quot;: &quot;A Sunday on La Grande Jatte — 1884&quot;,
        &quot;image_id&quot;: &quot;1adf2696-8489-499b-cad2-821d7fde4b33&quot;
    },
    &quot;config&quot;: {
        &quot;iiif_url&quot;: &quot;https://www.artic.edu/iiif/2&quot;,
    }
}
</code></pre></div></li> <li><p>Find the base IIIF Image API endpoint in the <code>config.iiif_url</code> field:</p> <div class="language- extra-class"><pre class="language-text"><code>https://www.artic.edu/iiif/2
</code></pre></div><p>We recommend that you avoid hardcoding this value into your applications.</p></li> <li><p>Append the <code>image_id</code> of the artwork as a segment to this URL:</p> <div class="language- extra-class"><pre class="language-text"><code>https://www.artic.edu/iiif/2/1adf2696-8489-499b-cad2-821d7fde4b33
</code></pre></div></li> <li><p>Append <code>/full/843,/0/default.jpg</code> to the URL:</p> <div class="language- extra-class"><pre class="language-text"><code>https://www.artic.edu/iiif/2/1adf2696-8489-499b-cad2-821d7fde4b33/full/843,/0/default.jpg
</code></pre></div></li></ol> <p>That's it! This is a valid IIIF URL. It will return the same image as we use on our website:</p> <p><img src="https://www.artic.edu/iiif/2/1adf2696-8489-499b-cad2-821d7fde4b33/full/843,/0/default.jpg" alt="La Grande Jatte"></p> <p>Some artworks also have <code>alt_image_ids</code>, such as the <a href="https://www.artic.edu/artworks/75644/coronation-stone-of-motecuhzoma-ii-stone-of-the-five-suns" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Coronation Stone of Motecuhzoma II<span><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" x="0px" y="0px" viewBox="0 0 100 100" width="15" height="15" class="icon outbound"><path fill="currentColor" d="M18.8,85.1h56l0,0c2.2,0,4-1.8,4-4v-32h-8v28h-48v-48h28v-8h-32l0,0c-2.2,0-4,1.8-4,4v56C14.8,83.3,16.6,85.1,18.8,85.1z"></path> <polygon fill="currentColor" points="45.7,48.7 51.3,54.3 77.2,28.5 77.2,37.2 85.2,37.2 85.2,14.9 62.8,14.9 62.8,22.9 71.5,22.9"></polygon></svg> <span class="sr-only">(opens new window)</span></span></a>:</p> <div class="language- extra-class"><pre class="language-text"><code>https://api.artic.edu/api/v1/artworks/75644?fields=id,title,image_id,alt_image_ids
</code></pre></div><p>Exhibitions have images, too:</p> <div class="language- extra-class"><pre class="language-text"><code>https://api.artic.edu/api/v1/exhibitions/4568?fields=id,title,image_id,alt_image_ids
</code></pre></div><h3 id="image-sizes"><a href="#image-sizes" class="header-anchor">#</a> Image Sizes</h3> <p>The <a href="https://iiif.io/api/image/2.0/" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">IIIF Image API 2.0<span><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" x="0px" y="0px" viewBox="0 0 100 100" width="15" height="15" class="icon outbound"><path fill="currentColor" d="M18.8,85.1h56l0,0c2.2,0,4-1.8,4-4v-32h-8v28h-48v-48h28v-8h-32l0,0c-2.2,0-4,1.8-4,4v56C14.8,83.3,16.6,85.1,18.8,85.1z"></path> <polygon fill="currentColor" points="45.7,48.7 51.3,54.3 77.2,28.5 77.2,37.2 85.2,37.2 85.2,14.9 62.8,14.9 62.8,22.9 71.5,22.9"></polygon></svg> <span class="sr-only">(opens new window)</span></span></a> has a lot of options. However, we prefer to use it conservatively. As mentioned above, here's the URL pattern we use for most projects:</p> <div class="language- extra-class"><pre class="language-text"><code>https://www.artic.edu/iiif/2/{identifier}/full/843,/0/default.jpg
</code></pre></div><p>We recommend using <code>/full/843,/0/default.jpg</code> because this is the most common size used by our website, so there's a good chance that the image has previously been requested and cached. If you use this pattern in your projects, it will make images load faster in your application and decrease load on our systems.</p> <div class="custom-block tip"><p class="custom-block-title">TIP</p> <p>We don't mind if you hotlink to our images. Our images support CORS with <code>Access-Control-Allow-Origin: *</code> headers—same as our API responses! However, please be aware that any image can get unpublished or replaced at any time.</p></div> <p>If you'd like to display the full image at smaller sizes, we recommend the following size increments, as these are likely to give you a cache hit as well:</p> <div class="language- extra-class"><pre class="language-text"><code>https://www.artic.edu/iiif/2/{identifier}/full/200,/0/default.jpg
https://www.artic.edu/iiif/2/{identifier}/full/400,/0/default.jpg
https://www.artic.edu/iiif/2/{identifier}/full/600,/0/default.jpg
https://www.artic.edu/iiif/2/{identifier}/full/843,/0/default.jpg
</code></pre></div><p>If you are accessing public domain images, you may also use the following pattern for larger images:</p> <div class="language- extra-class"><pre class="language-text"><code>https://www.artic.edu/iiif/2/{identifier}/full/1686,/0/default.jpg
</code></pre></div><p>However, we still recommend using <code>843</code> instead unless there's a clear need for <code>1686</code>.</p> <p>Why the strange number—843px wide? This number is related to certain <a href="https://aamd.org/sites/default/files/document/Guidelines%20for%20the%20Use%20of%20Copyrighted%20Materials.pdf" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">guidelines for the use of copyrighted materials<span><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" x="0px" y="0px" viewBox="0 0 100 100" width="15" height="15" class="icon outbound"><path fill="currentColor" d="M18.8,85.1h56l0,0c2.2,0,4-1.8,4-4v-32h-8v28h-48v-48h28v-8h-32l0,0c-2.2,0-4,1.8-4,4v56C14.8,83.3,16.6,85.1,18.8,85.1z"></path> <polygon fill="currentColor" points="45.7,48.7 51.3,54.3 77.2,28.5 77.2,37.2 85.2,37.2 85.2,14.9 62.8,14.9 62.8,22.9 71.5,22.9"></polygon></svg> <span class="sr-only">(opens new window)</span></span></a> within the museum field. Over time, it became the most common dimension used by our applications, so it is the size we continue to recommend.</p> <h3 id="iiif-manifests"><a href="#iiif-manifests" class="header-anchor">#</a> IIIF Manifests</h3> <p>We also offer IIIF Manifests for all of our public domain artworks. A manifest is a resource defined by the <a href="https://iiif.io/api/presentation/2.0/" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">IIIF Presentation API<span><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" x="0px" y="0px" viewBox="0 0 100 100" width="15" height="15" class="icon outbound"><path fill="currentColor" d="M18.8,85.1h56l0,0c2.2,0,4-1.8,4-4v-32h-8v28h-48v-48h28v-8h-32l0,0c-2.2,0-4,1.8-4,4v56C14.8,83.3,16.6,85.1,18.8,85.1z"></path> <polygon fill="currentColor" points="45.7,48.7 51.3,54.3 77.2,28.5 77.2,37.2 85.2,37.2 85.2,14.9 62.8,14.9 62.8,22.9 71.5,22.9"></polygon></svg> <span class="sr-only">(opens new window)</span></span></a>. Each manifest contains artwork metadata—such as title, artist name, and copyright info—alongside a list of images associated with that artwork.</p> <p>You can access the manifest by appending <code>/manifest.json</code> after the identifier in an artwork detail endpoint. For example, here is the manifest for <a href="https://www.artic.edu/artworks/24645/under-the-wave-off-kanagawa-kanagawa-oki-nami-ura-also-known-as-the-great-wave-from-the-series-thirty-six-views-of-mount-fuji-fugaku-sanjurokkei" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">The Great Wave<span><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" x="0px" y="0px" viewBox="0 0 100 100" width="15" height="15" class="icon outbound"><path fill="currentColor" d="M18.8,85.1h56l0,0c2.2,0,4-1.8,4-4v-32h-8v28h-48v-48h28v-8h-32l0,0c-2.2,0-4,1.8-4,4v56C14.8,83.3,16.6,85.1,18.8,85.1z"></path> <polygon fill="currentColor" points="45.7,48.7 51.3,54.3 77.2,28.5 77.2,37.2 85.2,37.2 85.2,14.9 62.8,14.9 62.8,22.9 71.5,22.9"></polygon></svg> <span class="sr-only">(opens new window)</span></span></a>:</p> <div class="language- extra-class"><pre class="language-text"><code>https://api.artic.edu/api/v1/artworks/24645/manifest.json
</code></pre></div><p>These IIIF manifests do not contain any data that cannot be found elsewhere in our API. But because they follow a standardized API, they can be used with IIIF-compliant tools, such as <a href="https://projectmirador.org/" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Mirador<span><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" x="0px" y="0px" viewBox="0 0 100 100" width="15" height="15" class="icon outbound"><path fill="currentColor" d="M18.8,85.1h56l0,0c2.2,0,4-1.8,4-4v-32h-8v28h-48v-48h28v-8h-32l0,0c-2.2,0-4,1.8-4,4v56C14.8,83.3,16.6,85.1,18.8,85.1z"></path> <polygon fill="currentColor" points="45.7,48.7 51.3,54.3 77.2,28.5 77.2,37.2 85.2,37.2 85.2,14.9 62.8,14.9 62.8,22.9 71.5,22.9"></polygon></svg> <span class="sr-only">(opens new window)</span></span></a>. For a fun example, try pasting the URL above into the <a href="https://experiments.getty.edu/ac-art-generator#iiifloader" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Getty's Animal Crossing Art Generator<span><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" x="0px" y="0px" viewBox="0 0 100 100" width="15" height="15" class="icon outbound"><path fill="currentColor" d="M18.8,85.1h56l0,0c2.2,0,4-1.8,4-4v-32h-8v28h-48v-48h28v-8h-32l0,0c-2.2,0-4,1.8-4,4v56C14.8,83.3,16.6,85.1,18.8,85.1z"></path> <polygon fill="currentColor" points="45.7,48.7 51.3,54.3 77.2,28.5 77.2,37.2 85.2,37.2 85.2,14.9 62.8,14.9 62.8,22.9 71.5,22.9"></polygon></svg> <span class="sr-only">(opens new window)</span></span></a>—and scroll up to see the result!</p> <h3 id="image-dumps"><a href="#image-dumps" class="header-anchor">#</a> Image Dumps</h3> <p>Unfortunately, we cannot offer image dumps at this time. We are exploring that possibility.</p> <p>For now, you may scrape images from our IIIF Image API. We ask that you please follow our guidelines for <a href="#scraping-images">scraping images</a> if you decide to do so.</p> <h3 id="scraping-images"><a href="#scraping-images" class="header-anchor">#</a> Scraping Images</h3> <p>Generally, we prefer that you hotlink to our images, rather than scraping them. Scraping images can put unnecessary strain on our systems. That said, we understand that many use-cases require images to be stored locally.</p> <p>If you'd like to scrape images from our IIIF API, please follow these guidelines:</p> <ul><li>Scrape one image at a time—do not use multiple threads or processes!</li> <li>Consider adding a delay of 1 second between each image download</li> <li>Use the following URL pattern to download our <a href="image-sizes">most common image size</a>:<div class="language- extra-class"><pre class="language-text"><code>https://www.artic.edu/iiif/2/{identifier}/full/843,/0/default.jpg
</code></pre></div></li></ul> <h3 id="copyright"><a href="#copyright" class="header-anchor">#</a> Copyright</h3> <p>For information about copyright, please see the <a href="https://www.artic.edu/image-licensing" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Image Licensing<span><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" x="0px" y="0px" viewBox="0 0 100 100" width="15" height="15" class="icon outbound"><path fill="currentColor" d="M18.8,85.1h56l0,0c2.2,0,4-1.8,4-4v-32h-8v28h-48v-48h28v-8h-32l0,0c-2.2,0-4,1.8-4,4v56C14.8,83.3,16.6,85.1,18.8,85.1z"></path> <polygon fill="currentColor" points="45.7,48.7 51.3,54.3 77.2,28.5 77.2,37.2 85.2,37.2 85.2,14.9 62.8,14.9 62.8,22.9 71.5,22.9"></polygon></svg> <span class="sr-only">(opens new window)</span></span></a> and <a href="https://www.artic.edu/terms" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Terms<span><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" x="0px" y="0px" viewBox="0 0 100 100" width="15" height="15" class="icon outbound"><path fill="currentColor" d="M18.8,85.1h56l0,0c2.2,0,4-1.8,4-4v-32h-8v28h-48v-48h28v-8h-32l0,0c-2.2,0-4,1.8-4,4v56C14.8,83.3,16.6,85.1,18.8,85.1z"></path> <polygon fill="currentColor" points="45.7,48.7 51.3,54.3 77.2,28.5 77.2,37.2 85.2,37.2 85.2,14.9 62.8,14.9 62.8,22.9 71.5,22.9"></polygon></svg> <span class="sr-only">(opens new window)</span></span></a> pages on our website. We defer to those resources in all legal respects.</p> <p>From a developer's perspective, we recommend only using images from artworks that are tagged as public domain. When querying for artworks, you can filter by public domain status like so:</p> <div class="language- extra-class"><pre class="language-text"><code>https://api.artic.edu/api/v1/artworks/search?query[term][is_public_domain]=true&amp;limit=0
</code></pre></div><p>To get the full list of artworks that are in the public domain, you will need to download our <a href="#data-dumps">data dumps</a> and perform the filtering locally.</p> <p>Please note that you may encounter images that are not public domain via our <a href="#iiif-image-api">IIIF Image API</a>. It is up to you to determine whether or not you are allowed to use these images for your use-case and to obtain any necessary permissions.</p> <h2 id="general-information"><a href="#general-information" class="header-anchor">#</a> General Information</h2> <h3 id="authentication"><a href="#authentication" class="header-anchor">#</a> Authentication</h3> <p>You may access our API without authentication. Anonymous users are throttled to 60 requests per minute. (Each IP counts as a separate user.) If you are working on an application that needs to exceed this restriction, please get in touch with us at <a href="mailto:engineering@artic.edu">engineering@artic.edu</a>.</p> <p>Please use <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/HTTPS" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">HTTPS<span><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" x="0px" y="0px" viewBox="0 0 100 100" width="15" height="15" class="icon outbound"><path fill="currentColor" d="M18.8,85.1h56l0,0c2.2,0,4-1.8,4-4v-32h-8v28h-48v-48h28v-8h-32l0,0c-2.2,0-4,1.8-4,4v56C14.8,83.3,16.6,85.1,18.8,85.1z"></path> <polygon fill="currentColor" points="45.7,48.7 51.3,54.3 77.2,28.5 77.2,37.2 85.2,37.2 85.2,14.9 62.8,14.9 62.8,22.9 71.5,22.9"></polygon></svg> <span class="sr-only">(opens new window)</span></span></a> to access our API. To support legacy applications, our API is currently accessible via both HTTP and HTTPS, but HTTP access will be removed in the future.</p> <p>Lastly, consider adding a <code>AIC-User-Agent</code> header with the name of your project and a contact email to your API requests. For example:</p> <div class="language-bash extra-class"><pre class="language-bash"><code><span class="token function">curl</span> <span class="token string">'https://api.artic.edu/api/v1/artworks/24645'</span> <span class="token punctuation">\</span>
--header <span class="token string">'AIC-User-Agent: aic-bash (engineering@artic.edu)'</span>
</code></pre></div><p>We ask that you do so as a <a href="https://towardsdatascience.com/ethics-in-web-scraping-b96b18136f01" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">matter of courtesy<span><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" x="0px" y="0px" viewBox="0 0 100 100" width="15" height="15" class="icon outbound"><path fill="currentColor" d="M18.8,85.1h56l0,0c2.2,0,4-1.8,4-4v-32h-8v28h-48v-48h28v-8h-32l0,0c-2.2,0-4,1.8-4,4v56C14.8,83.3,16.6,85.1,18.8,85.1z"></path> <polygon fill="currentColor" points="45.7,48.7 51.3,54.3 77.2,28.5 77.2,37.2 85.2,37.2 85.2,14.9 62.8,14.9 62.8,22.9 71.5,22.9"></polygon></svg> <span class="sr-only">(opens new window)</span></span></a>. If we see an application using a disproportionate amount of system resources, this gives us an avenue to reach out and work with you to optimize your queries.</p> <div class="custom-block tip"><p class="custom-block-title">TIP</p> <p>Why <code>AIC-User-Agent</code>? Because modifying the <code>User-Agent</code> header is <a href="https://stackoverflow.com/questions/42815087/sending-a-custom-user-agent-string-along-with-my-headers-fetch" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">forbidden by some browsers<span><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" x="0px" y="0px" viewBox="0 0 100 100" width="15" height="15" class="icon outbound"><path fill="currentColor" d="M18.8,85.1h56l0,0c2.2,0,4-1.8,4-4v-32h-8v28h-48v-48h28v-8h-32l0,0c-2.2,0-4,1.8-4,4v56C14.8,83.3,16.6,85.1,18.8,85.1z"></path> <polygon fill="currentColor" points="45.7,48.7 51.3,54.3 77.2,28.5 77.2,37.2 85.2,37.2 85.2,14.9 62.8,14.9 62.8,22.9 71.5,22.9"></polygon></svg> <span class="sr-only">(opens new window)</span></span></a>. We are using the <code>AIC-</code> namespace to reduce potential conflicts with other applications (<a href="https://datatracker.ietf.org/doc/html/rfc6648" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">RFC 6648<span><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" x="0px" y="0px" viewBox="0 0 100 100" width="15" height="15" class="icon outbound"><path fill="currentColor" d="M18.8,85.1h56l0,0c2.2,0,4-1.8,4-4v-32h-8v28h-48v-48h28v-8h-32l0,0c-2.2,0-4,1.8-4,4v56C14.8,83.3,16.6,85.1,18.8,85.1z"></path> <polygon fill="currentColor" points="45.7,48.7 51.3,54.3 77.2,28.5 77.2,37.2 85.2,37.2 85.2,14.9 62.8,14.9 62.8,22.9 71.5,22.9"></polygon></svg> <span class="sr-only">(opens new window)</span></span></a>).</p></div> <h3 id="pagination"><a href="#pagination" class="header-anchor">#</a> Pagination</h3> <p>Listing and search endpoints are paginated. We show 12 records per page by default. Pagination can be controlled via the following query parameters:</p> <ul><li><code>page</code> to request a specific page of results (1-based, default: 1)</li> <li><code>limit</code> to set how many records each page should return (0-based, default: 12)</li></ul> <p><strong>Example:</strong> <a href="https://api.artic.edu/api/v1/artists?page=2&amp;limit=10" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">https://api.artic.edu/api/v1/artists?page=2&amp;limit=10<span><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" x="0px" y="0px" viewBox="0 0 100 100" width="15" height="15" class="icon outbound"><path fill="currentColor" d="M18.8,85.1h56l0,0c2.2,0,4-1.8,4-4v-32h-8v28h-48v-48h28v-8h-32l0,0c-2.2,0-4,1.8-4,4v56C14.8,83.3,16.6,85.1,18.8,85.1z"></path> <polygon fill="currentColor" points="45.7,48.7 51.3,54.3 77.2,28.5 77.2,37.2 85.2,37.2 85.2,14.9 62.8,14.9 62.8,22.9 71.5,22.9"></polygon></svg> <span class="sr-only">(opens new window)</span></span></a></p> <p>For performance reasons, <code>limit</code> cannot exceed 100. Additionally, you cannot request more than 10,000 records from a single search query through any combination of <code>limit</code> and <code>page</code>. For example:</p> <ul><li><strong>Success:</strong> <a href="https://api.artic.edu/api/v1/artists/search?limit=100&amp;page=100" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">https://api.artic.edu/api/v1/artists/search?limit=100&amp;page=100<span><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" x="0px" y="0px" viewBox="0 0 100 100" width="15" height="15" class="icon outbound"><path fill="currentColor" d="M18.8,85.1h56l0,0c2.2,0,4-1.8,4-4v-32h-8v28h-48v-48h28v-8h-32l0,0c-2.2,0-4,1.8-4,4v56C14.8,83.3,16.6,85.1,18.8,85.1z"></path> <polygon fill="currentColor" points="45.7,48.7 51.3,54.3 77.2,28.5 77.2,37.2 85.2,37.2 85.2,14.9 62.8,14.9 62.8,22.9 71.5,22.9"></polygon></svg> <span class="sr-only">(opens new window)</span></span></a></li> <li><strong>Error:</strong> <a href="https://api.artic.edu/api/v1/artists/search?limit=100&amp;page=101" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">https://api.artic.edu/api/v1/artists/search?limit=100&amp;page=101<span><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" x="0px" y="0px" viewBox="0 0 100 100" width="15" height="15" class="icon outbound"><path fill="currentColor" d="M18.8,85.1h56l0,0c2.2,0,4-1.8,4-4v-32h-8v28h-48v-48h28v-8h-32l0,0c-2.2,0-4,1.8-4,4v56C14.8,83.3,16.6,85.1,18.8,85.1z"></path> <polygon fill="currentColor" points="45.7,48.7 51.3,54.3 77.2,28.5 77.2,37.2 85.2,37.2 85.2,14.9 62.8,14.9 62.8,22.9 71.5,22.9"></polygon></svg> <span class="sr-only">(opens new window)</span></span></a></li></ul> <p>Generally, we ask that you avoid scraping our API. If you want to filter and scrape our search results, but the number of results is greater than 10,000, you won't be able to scrape them fully. Instead, we recommend that you download our <a href="#data-dumps">data dumps</a> and perform your filtering locally.</p> <p>Occasionally, it might be useful to check the total number of results without retrieving any data. Our API supports this by allowing you to make a request with <code>limit=0</code>. For example, check <code>pagination.total</code> of this query to see the number of public domain artworks in our API:</p> <p><a href="https://api.artic.edu/api/v1/artworks/search?query%5Bterm%5D%5Bis_public_domain%5D=true&amp;limit=0" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">https://api.artic.edu/api/v1/artworks/search?query[term][is_public_domain]=true&amp;limit=0<span><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" x="0px" y="0px" viewBox="0 0 100 100" width="15" height="15" class="icon outbound"><path fill="currentColor" d="M18.8,85.1h56l0,0c2.2,0,4-1.8,4-4v-32h-8v28h-48v-48h28v-8h-32l0,0c-2.2,0-4,1.8-4,4v56C14.8,83.3,16.6,85.1,18.8,85.1z"></path> <polygon fill="currentColor" points="45.7,48.7 51.3,54.3 77.2,28.5 77.2,37.2 85.2,37.2 85.2,14.9 62.8,14.9 62.8,22.9 71.5,22.9"></polygon></svg> <span class="sr-only">(opens new window)</span></span></a></p> <p>All paginated endpoints include a <code>pagination</code> block in their responses:</p> <div class="language-json extra-class"><pre class="language-json"><code><span class="token punctuation">{</span>

Artworks in the groups of essentials are boosted so they'll show up higher in results.</p> <h6 id="available-parameters-2"><a href="#available-parameters-2" class="header-anchor">#</a> Available parameters:</h6> <ul><li><code>q</code> - Your search query</li> <li><code>query</code> - For complex queries, you can pass Elasticsearch domain syntax queries here</li> <li><code>sort</code> - Used in conjunction with <code>query</code></li> <li><code>from</code> - Starting point of results. Pagination via Elasticsearch conventions</li> <li><code>size</code> - Number of results to return. Pagination via Elasticsearch conventions</li> <li><code>facets</code> - A comma-separated list of 'count' aggregation facets to include in the results.</li></ul> <details class="custom-block details"><summary>Example request: https://api.artic.edu/api/v1/artworks/search?q=monet</summary> <div class="language-js extra-class"><pre class="language-js"><code><span class="token punctuation">{</span>

            <span class="token string">&quot;transcript&quot;</span><span class="token operator">:</span> <span class="token string">&quot;&lt;p&gt;Narrator:&lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;p&gt;Curator Costa Petridis&lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;p&gt;Costa Petridis:&lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;p&gt;So one way to approach this object is through its local name, which is nkishi. Nkishi is the local word for what we would typically refer to today as a power figure or a power object. And so what these objects are, literally are figures that serve as the receptacles of very powerful ingredients or substances that are literally contained within the object. And here among the Songye, as in many other parts in Southern Congo and elsewhere in Africa, those substances or ingredients are typically a concoction of materials from the animal world, from the plant world, from the mineral world, from the human world: nail clippings, bits of hair, stones that are found in nature, pieces of wood and bark, but also saliva, blood of animals that are offered, that are brewed together literally into a concoction, that is then inserted in specific body cavities. The belly is typically hollowed out and then contains these ingredients and then also the top of the skull.&lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;p&gt;They're owned by leaders, by chiefs for the benefit of their community. They're invoked in order to deal with problems, conflicts such as epidemics, the threat of war in particular, but also the potential threat of metaphysical problems, like witches or other evildoers that harm the wellbeing of the community. These objects, in some ways, transcend the divide between the beautiful and the ugly, where the beauty of the carving, the powerful carving that represents an ancestor, it imitates all these attributes. And insignia of power and leadership is combined with a very powerful entity, with something that is embedded in the sculpture, and that because of its power, has both positive and negative connotations, both defensive and offensive qualities. And so the idea is that in these large-scale communities, Songye power figures, you have a combination of the beautiful and the ugly in one, in a new aesthetic category that I've come to label awesome art because it creates awe in the minds and in the eyes of the people who are confronted with these objects.&lt;/p&gt;\n&quot;</span><span class="token punctuation">,</span>
            <span class="token operator">...</span>
        <span class="token punctuation">}</span>

Van Dyck\u2019s self-portrait is probably the first unfinished print ever to have been produced in an edition.&lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;p&gt;VICTORIA SANCHO LOBIS: Like most of the etchings that Van Dyck made, this print shows the effects of an imperfectly polished copper plate. Pagination via Elasticsearch conventions</li> <li><code>size</code> - Number of results to return. Pagination via Elasticsearch conventions</li> <li><code>facets</code> - A comma-separated list of 'count' aggregation facets to include in the results.</li></ul> <details class="custom-block details"><summary>Example request: https://api.artic.edu/api/v1/publications/search</summary> <div class="language-js extra-class"><pre class="language-js"><code><span class="token punctuation">{</span>

            <span class="token string">&quot;api_model&quot;</span><span class="token operator">:</span> <span class="token string">&quot;articles&quot;</span><span class="token punctuation">,</span>
            <span class="token string">&quot;api_link&quot;</span><span class="token operator">:</span> <span class="token string">&quot;https://api.artic.edu/api/v1/articles/1029&quot;</span><span class="token punctuation">,</span>
            <span class="token string">&quot;title&quot;</span><span class="token operator">:</span> <span class="token string">&quot;Bessie Potter Vonnoh, Trailblazing Chicago Sculptor&quot;</span><span class="token punctuation">,</span>
            <span class="token string">&quot;copy&quot;</span><span class="token operator">:</span> <span class="token string">&quot; Her remarkable career, like those of many women artists, eventually faded from view. Let\u2019s look anew at Vonnoh (1872\u20131955).   In 1895 the museum acquired seven of Vonnoh's sculptures: singular female figures (save for one of a male) in contemporary dress, about 12 to 15 inches in height, including The Chrysanthemum Girl. Cast in plaster, the compositions were conceived to be finished works in a material oftentimes employed by artists as an intermediate step\u2014a sketch for a composition yet to be refined.   Vonnoh, in contrast, embraced plaster as an accessible medium with possibilities. It captured the rough, freely modeled surfaces of her statuettes (dubbed \u201cPotterines\u201d in her day), suggestive and appealingly tactile in form. For French artist Jean-Fran\u00e7ois Rafa\u00eblli, her three-dimensional works resonated with the canvases of modern plein-air painters. Vonnoh was, in his words, \u201can impressionist in plaster.\u201d   Today, her name remains unfamiliar to many art enthusiasts and scholars, but at the turn of the 20th century, Bessie Potter Vonnoh made headlines. A total of nine of her sculptures were in the Art Institute\u2019s holdings by 1901. And in 1914\u201315, the museum mounted a solo exhibition on the artist. A number of sculptures\u2014this time bronze casts\u2014were purchased by the museum out of the exhibition.   Vonnoh was born Bessie Onahotema Potter in St. Louis in 1872, her middle name meaning \u201cshe gives with an open hand\u201d in the language of the Choctaw Nation. (Vonnoh was white; her mother sought out a Native name, according to the artist.) A short time later, after the death of her father, she and her mother moved to Chicago. Vonnoh suffered from an unknown illness as a young child. The prescribed medical treatments left her immobile much of the time, which greatly stunted her growth, although she eventually regained her health. Years later, as she garnered artistic fame, much was made in the press about her diminutive stature in relation to her big talents.   Her achievements as a young female artist were considerable. As a schoolgirl in Chicago, sculpture drew her in. In 1886 at age 14, Vonnoh was already a student at the School of the Art Institute of Chicago, working in the studio of sculptor and instructor Lorado Taft. Five years later, while wrapping up coursework, she stepped into the art world as a professional, exhibiting a portrait bust at the museum\u2019s Fourth Annual Exhibition of American Oil Paintings and Sculpture . Vonnoh had arrived.   Her path to success was certainly noteworthy, but Vonnoh was not alone. In late 19th-century Chicago, networks of and opportunities for (largely white) female professionals were on the rise. Women studied at the School of the Art Institute in strong numbers at the time of Vonnoh\u2019s tenure. In 1892 female students surpassed male students six to one. And the school\u2019s faculty mirrored that drive toward professionalism: painter Alice Kellogg (later Tyler), a graduate of the program, became an instructor there in 1881, and by the following decade women comprised almost one-third of the faculty. Women artists were on the rosters of early exhibitions at the Art Institute, including various annuals for contemporary American art, Chicago artists, and students of the school. (To be sure, the percentage of female exhibitors remained small, but a nexus of artists demonstrated that it could be done, despite persistent obstacles.) Additionally, women in Chicago created their own organizations, such as the Bohemian Art Club (later the Palette Club), whose annual exhibitions were held at the museum beginning in 1883\u2014just four years after the founding of the Art Institute itself.   Her training completed, Vonnoh seized an important opportunity: executing public commissions in sculpture for the city\u2019s 1893 World\u2019s Columbian Exposition. She, along with a cadre of local female sculptors including Julia Bracken (later Wendt) and Janet Scudder, who had likewise studied with Taft, assisted on projects for the fair\u2019s Horticulture Building. Additionally, Vonnoh made her mark with individual contributions to the Illinois State Building and the Fine Arts exhibition.   Establishing a studio in 1894, Vonnoh carved out a niche in Chicago\u2019s artistic life, honing in on what would become her celebrated forms: domestically scaled sculptures of contemporary women. Rapidly sketched, they were genre studies as well as portraits, evoking a fleeting moment or a familiar activity.   In the mid-1890s, Vonnoh\u2019s sculptures were in high demand. She could produce plaster casts from her clay models in large editions and offer them at prices accessible to middle-class patrons. The artist experimented with the finish of her plasters through tinting\u2014applying soft, matte color to portions of an object to heighten the visual interest of its active surfaces, as she did in An American Girl .   In 1895 the seven plasters by Vonnoh that entered the Art Institute were The Chrysanthemum Girl , An American Girl , Miss F. , William , Mildred , Evelyn , and A Summer Girl (the first three were tinted sculptures). The set was purchased and presented to the museum by the Arch\u00e9 Club, a local women\u2019s group dedicated to the study of art and history. Records for the acquisition note, \u201cThe Arch\u00e9 Club gave $100 to be devoted to the work of a woman sculptor in Chicago.\u201d Here again is an example of a (modest) structure of support for female professionals in the city in the late 19th century.   With these acquisitions, Vonnoh became the first named woman sculptor\u2014and only the second woman artist\u2014represented at the Art Institute (Annie C. Shaw was the first, with paintings acquired in 1892 and 1894). Neither Rosa Bonheur nor Mary Cassatt, for instance\u2014artists whose names and reputations have fared much better over the last century\u2014had a work in the permanent collection yet (not until 1901 and 1910, respectively).   After her early achievements in Chicago, Bessie Potter Vonnoh set her sights further afield, traveling abroad and eventually settling in New York, where she married painter Robert Vonnoh in 1899. Her pace did not slow. Attracting patrons with deeper pockets, she was then able to translate her sculptures into bronze, a new challenge.   Her 1914\u201315 exhibition at the Art Institute featured thirty-three sculptures, largely in bronze. Examples of her work in this medium are what can be found in the collection today. Sadly, none of her plasters remain at the Art Institute. When seven bronzes were purchased in 1915, Vonnoh requested that her earlier plasters be returned, and the museum obliged. We can assume that the artist felt the bronzes better represented her mature work. The Art Institute currently holds five of her sculptures.   Vonnoh\u2019s fascinating story comes to light when we take time to experience her sculptures and comb the archives. The dividends are many: we come to understand better our own institutional histories, elevate and celebrate objects too long neglected, and carve a different path forward in the galleries. \u2014Annelise K. Madsen, Gilda and Henry Buchbinder Associate Curator, Arts of the Americas &quot;</span><span class="token punctuation">,</span>

            <span class="token operator">...</span>
        <span class="token punctuation">}</span><span class="token punctuation">,</span>

            <span class="token string">&quot;api_link&quot;</span><span class="token operator">:</span> <span class="token string">&quot;https://api.artic.edu/api/v1/articles/705&quot;</span><span class="token punctuation">,</span>
            <span class="token string">&quot;title&quot;</span><span class="token operator">:</span> <span class="token string">&quot;Hidden Materials in John Singer Sargent\u2019s Watercolors&quot;</span><span class="token punctuation">,</span>
            <span class="token string">&quot;copy&quot;</span><span class="token operator">:</span> <span class="token string">&quot; While John Singer Sargent is most widely known for his oil portraits of august men and women in fashionable interiors, he cultivated a love of painting outdoors from an early age. As a boy he recorded his family\u2019s European travels in sketchbooks, and as his talent and repertoire grew, he acquired numerous accoutrements such as portable easels, sketching umbrellas, rigid pads of paper, and compact palettes of watercolors that allowed him to paint multiple pictures during one outing, even in challenging conditions. In fact, Sargent was an official war artist for Britain during World War I and spent four months on the front painting and sketching.   A fellow war artist, Henry Tonks, painted this watercolor caricature of Sargent in 1918, depicting the artist clothed in army greens and shielded by a sketching umbrella that Sargent camouflaged for the purpose. The painting (held in the collection of the Museum of Fine Arts in Boston, and not a part of this exhibition) gives new meaning to challenging conditions\u2014and shows us a glimpse of Sargent\u2019s life apart from glamorous portraits.   In preparation for the current exhibition John Singer Sargent and Chicago\u2019s Gilded Age , Art Institute curators, conservators, and conservation scientists examined some of Sargent\u2019s paintings and investigated his less obvious materials, finding evidence that provides valuable insight into the artist\u2019s working process. A Newsworthy Surprise Sargent captured hundreds of landscapes in watercolor as he traveled across Europe and North America. In 1908 he painted Tarragona Terrace and Garden when he visited the eastern coast of Spain. Seated in the arcade of Tarragona\u2019s cathedral, Sargent made a quick study of its columns.   While he generally preferred to leave parts of the paper bare to delineate highlights, the foliage in the upper left corner of this picture was painted using a different technique. Here it appears that Sargent simply laid in a mass of greens and browns and then returned with an opaque, zinc white paint to create his highlights. In order to fully conceal the dark colors underneath, Sargent had to use thick dabs of white as if he were making a correction in oils. Sargent often made multiple paintings in one day and would interleave his paintings with sheets of newspaper for protection as he carried them. He did this with Tarragona Terrace and Garden , perhaps not realizing that the thickly applied areas of paint had not dried completely when he laid the newspaper on its surface. As an unintended consequence, fragments from a Spanish newspaper stuck to the painting, remnants of Sargent\u2019s panting process that survive today.   In normal light these tiny pieces of newsprint are barely noticeable, but they stand out in an infrared photograph, which makes some of the Spanish text almost legible.   Wax in a Watercolor Nearly 10 years after he painted Tarragona Terrace and Garden , Sargent made another series of stunning architectural studies while visiting his friends Charles and James Deering in Florida. Sargent was drawn to Vizcaya, the lavish estate that James had recently built, not least of all because it reminded him of the Italian landscapes and gardens that he loved to paint.   Analytical instruments in the conservation science lab at the Art Institute can help answer a lot of questions about artists\u2019 materials. In the case of this work, scientists sought more information about a soft, translucent material found in discrete areas on its surface. The material was analyzed and determined to be a wax, which Sargent used as a \u201cresist\u201d\u2014meaning that he marked the paper with a transparent material that would repel the water-based paint and leave highlights in the composition.   Analysis also revealed that the wax is a type called spermaceti, a product obtained from sperm whales and a major commercial product of the whaling industry. In Sargent\u2019s time this wax was commonly used to make candles. Finding it here helps to explain Sargent\u2019s process\u2014because spermaceti is softer than other common waxes such as beeswax, it would have been the logical choice for use as a drawing material. To learn more about Sargent\u2019s process and materials come visit John Singer Sargent and Chicago\u2019s Gilded Age in the Art Institute\u2019s Regenstein Hall through September 30, and check out the technical essay in the exhibition catalogue . \u2014Mary Broadway, associate conservator of prints and drawings &quot;</span><span class="token punctuation">,</span>

            <span class="token operator">...</span>

        <span class="token string">&quot;copy&quot;</span><span class="token operator">:</span> <span class="token string">&quot; I give a lot of tours of the Modern Wing, and there are details about the building that most visitors like but that aren\u2019t necessarily apparent to anyone going through the building on their own. So, here are some \u201csecrets\u201d of the Modern Wing. The building that now sits on Monroe Street is actually the third version of the expansion that the museum planned. We started thinking about expanding in 1999, before Millennium Park was built. So the original idea was to put the expansion on the south side of the building, over the railroad tracks. But once Millennium Park started to become more than parking lots, broken bottles, and train tracks, the architect Renzo Piano and museum leaders decided to completely reorient the building to face north. This move was made in 2001. To \u201ctalk\u201d to the park, and to test some proportional ideas for the fa\u00e7ade, Piano designed the two Exelon Pavilions across the street from the Modern Wing. You may know these pavilions as the entrances to the parking garages under the park. Same materials, same ideas as those for the Modern Wing. Modest structures, big architect.   A guiding principle for the Modern Wing is Piano\u2019s idea of \u201czero gravity\u201d\u2014that buildings should appear to levitate and lift. I had always heard about this idea, and I sense it when I\u2019m in the building, but it was never quite sure of how the details\u2014beyond lots of verticals\u2014worked. But the key to it in the Modern Wing is that everything is designed to not quite meet the floor. Every wall has a one-inch \u201creveal\u201d at the bottom of it. Piano designed all the benches, and they all sit slightly up off the floor on little pegs.   Every sculpture pedestal and platform also sit up off the floor. The main staircase also \u201cfloats,\u201d with an inch between what appears to be its base and the floor. Tiny detail, huge impact.   More to come! \u2014Erin Hogan &quot;</span><span class="token punctuation">,</span>

You may use this data for noncommercial educational and personal use and for &quot;fair use&quot; as authorized under law, provided that you also retain all copyright and other proprietary notices contained on the materials and cite the author and source of the materials.</em></p> <h5 id="get-highlights"><a href="#get-highlights" class="header-anchor">#</a> <code>GET /highlights</code></h5> <p>A list of all highlights sorted by last updated date in descending order. For a description of all the fields included with this response, see <a href="#highlights-2">here</a>.</p> <h6 id="available-parameters-46"><a href="#available-parameters-46" class="header-anchor">#</a> Available parameters:</h6> <ul><li><code>ids</code> - A comma-separated list of resource ids to retrieve</li> <li><code>limit</code> - The number of resources to return per page</li> <li><code>page</code> - The page of resources to retrieve</li> <li><code>fields</code> - A comma-separated list of fields to return per resource</li></ul> <details class="custom-block details"><summary>Example request: https://api.artic.edu/api/v1/highlights?limit=2</summary> <div class="language-js extra-class"><pre class="language-js"><code><span class="token punctuation">{</span>

        <span class="token string">&quot;limit&quot;</span><span class="token operator">:</span> <span class="token number">2</span><span class="token punctuation">,</span>

        <span class="token string">&quot;next_url&quot;</span><span class="token operator">:</span> <span class="token string">&quot;https://api.artic.edu/api/v1/highlights?page=2&amp;limit=2&quot;</span>
